<p>Montgomery returned to Britain in January 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> He was assigned to command the <a href="/wiki/21st_Army_Group" title="21st Army Group">21st Army Group</a> consisting of all Allied ground forces participating in <a href="/wiki/Operation_Overlord" title="Operation Overlord">Operation Overlord</a>, codename for the Allied invasion of <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a>. Overall direction was assigned to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Allied_Commander" title="Supreme Allied Commander">Supreme Allied Commander</a> of the Allied Expeditionary Forces, American <a href="/wiki/General_(United_States)" title="General (United States)">General</a> <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-heath217_90-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heath217-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Both Churchill and Eisenhower had found Montgomery difficult to work with in the past and wanted the position to go to the more affable General <a href="/wiki/Harold_Alexander,_1st_Earl_Alexander_of_Tunis" title="Harold Alexander, 1st Earl Alexander of Tunis">Sir Harold Alexander</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Keegan,_1994_p._56_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keegan,_1994_p._56-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> However Montgomery's patron, General Sir Alan Brooke, firmly argued that Montgomery was a much superior general to Alexander and ensured his appointment.<sup id="cite_ref-Keegan,_1994_p._56_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keegan,_1994_p._56-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Without Brooke's support, Montgomery would have remained in Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-Keegan,_1994_p._56_92-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keegan,_1994_p._56-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_School_(London)" class="mw-redirect" title="St Paul&#39;s School (London)">St Paul's School</a> on 7 April and 15 May Montgomery presented his strategy for the invasion. He envisaged a ninety-day battle, with all forces reaching the <a href="/wiki/Seine" title="Seine">Seine</a>. The campaign would pivot on an Allied-held <a href="/wiki/Caen" title="Caen">Caen</a> in the east of the Normandy bridgehead, with relatively static British and Canadian armies forming a shoulder to attract and defeat German counter-attacks, relieving the US armies who would move and seize the <a href="/wiki/Cotentin_Peninsula" title="Cotentin Peninsula">Cotentin Peninsula</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, wheeling south and then east on the right forming a pincer.<sup id="cite_ref-odnb_86-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-odnb-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_British_Army_in_the_United_Kingdom_1939-45_H35960.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/The_British_Army_in_the_United_Kingdom_1939-45_H35960.jpg/220px-The_British_Army_in_the_United_Kingdom_1939-45_H35960.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/The_British_Army_in_the_United_Kingdom_1939-45_H35960.jpg/330px-The_British_Army_in_the_United_Kingdom_1939-45_H35960.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/The_British_Army_in_the_United_Kingdom_1939-45_H35960.jpg/440px-The_British_Army_in_the_United_Kingdom_1939-45_H35960.jpg 2x" data-file-width="789" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_British_Army_in_the_United_Kingdom_1939-45_H35960.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>General Montgomery inspects men of the 5th/7th Battalion, <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Highlanders" title="Gordon Highlanders">Gordon Highlanders</a> of the <a href="/wiki/51st_(Highland)_Division" title="51st (Highland) Division">51st (Highland) Division</a>, at Beaconsfield, February 1944.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Simonds.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Simonds.jpg/220px-Simonds.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Simonds.jpg/330px-Simonds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Simonds.jpg/440px-Simonds.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="794" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Simonds.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Montgomery with officers of the <a href="/wiki/First_Canadian_Army" title="First Canadian Army">First Canadian Army</a>. From left, <a href="/wiki/Major_general" title="Major general">Major-General</a> <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Vokes" title="Christopher Vokes">Vokes</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_(Canada)" title="General (Canada)">General</a> <a href="/wiki/Harry_Crerar" title="Harry Crerar">Crerar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Field_marshal_(United_Kingdom)" title="Field marshal (United Kingdom)">Field Marshal</a> Montgomery, <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant-general_(United_Kingdom)" title="Lieutenant-general (United Kingdom)">Lieutenant-General</a> <a href="/wiki/Brian_Horrocks" title="Brian Horrocks">Horrocks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant-general_(Canada)" title="Lieutenant-general (Canada)">Lieutenant-General</a> <a href="/wiki/Guy_Simonds" title="Guy Simonds">Simonds</a>, Major-General Spry, and Major-General <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Matthews_(Canadian_Army_officer)" title="Bruce Matthews (Canadian Army officer)">Matthews</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>During the ten weeks of the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Normandy" title="Invasion of Normandy">Battle of Normandy</a>, unfavourable autumnal weather conditions disrupted the Normandy landing areas.<sup id="cite_ref-odnb_86-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-odnb-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Montgomery's initial plan was for the Anglo-Canadian troops under his command to break out immediately from their beachheads on the Calvados coast towards Caen with the aim of taking the city on either D Day or two days later.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Montgomery attempted to take Caen with the 3rd Infantry Division, <a href="/wiki/50th_(Northumbrian)_Infantry_Division" title="50th (Northumbrian) Infantry Division">50th (Northumbrian) Infantry Division</a> and the 3rd Canadian Division but was stopped from 6–8 June by 21st Panzer Division and <a href="/wiki/12th_SS_Panzer_Division_Hitlerjugend" title="12th SS Panzer Division Hitlerjugend">12th SS Panzer Division <i>Hitlerjugend</i></a>, who hit the advancing Anglo-Canadian troops very hard.<sup id="cite_ref-badsey43_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-badsey43-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Rommel followed up this success by ordering the 2nd Panzer Division to Caen while Field Marshal <a href="/wiki/Gerd_von_Rundstedt" title="Gerd von Rundstedt">Gerd von Rundstedt</a> asked for and received permission from Hitler to have the elite 1st Waffen SS Division <i>Leibstandarte Adolf Hitler</i> and 2nd Waffen SS Division <i>Das Reich</i> sent to Caen as well.<sup id="cite_ref-badsey43_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-badsey43-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Montgomery thus had to face what Stephen Badsey called the "most formidable" of all the German divisions in France.<sup id="cite_ref-badsey43_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-badsey43-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The 12th Waffen SS Division <i>Hitlerjugend</i> as its name implies was drawn entirely from the more fanatical elements of the Hitler Youth and commanded by the ruthless SS-<i>Brigadeführer</i> <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Meyer" title="Kurt Meyer">Kurt Meyer</a>, aka "Panzer Meyer".<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B5179.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B5179.jpg/220px-The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B5179.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B5179.jpg/330px-The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B5179.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B5179.jpg/440px-The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B5179.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="790" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B5179.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>General Montgomery passes German POWs while being driven along a road in a jeep, shortly after arriving in Normandy, 8 June 1944.</div></div></div>
<p>The failure to take Caen immediately has been the source of an immense historiographical dispute with bitter nationalist overtones.<sup id="cite_ref-Powers_pages_455-471_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Powers_pages_455-471-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Broadly, there has been a "British school" which accepts Montgomery's post-war claim that he never intended to take Caen at once, and instead the Anglo-Canadian operations around Caen were a "holding operation" intended to attract the bulk of the German forces towards the Caen sector to allow the Americans to stage the "break out operation" on the left flank of the German positions, which was all part of Montgomery's "Master Plan" that he had conceived long before the Normandy campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-Powers_pages_455-471_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Powers_pages_455-471-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> By contrast, the "American school" argued that Montgomery's initial "master plan" was for the 21st Army Group to take Caen at once, move his tank divisions into the plains south of Caen to stage a breakout that would lead the 21st Army Group into the plains of northern France and hence into <a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">Antwerp</a> and finally the <a href="/wiki/Ruhr" title="Ruhr">Ruhr</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Letters written by Eisenhower at the time of the battle make it clear that Eisenhower was expecting from Montgomery "the early capture of the important focal point of Caen". Later, when this plan had clearly failed, Eisenhower wrote that Montgomery had "evolved" the plan to have the US forces achieve the break-out instead.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B5787.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B5787.jpg/220px-The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B5787.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B5787.jpg/330px-The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B5787.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B5787.jpg/440px-The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B5787.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="782" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B5787.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>General Montgomery in conversation with Major General <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Graham_(British_Army_officer)" title="Douglas Graham (British Army officer)">Douglas Graham</a>, GOC <a href="/wiki/50th_(Northumbrian)_Infantry_Division" title="50th (Northumbrian) Infantry Division">50th (Northumbrian) Infantry Division</a>, pictured here in Normandy, 20 June 1944.</div></div></div>
<p>As the campaign progressed, Montgomery altered his initial plan for the invasion and continued the strategy of attracting and holding German counter-attacks in the area north of Caen rather than to the south, to allow the US First Army in the west to take Cherbourg. A memo summarising Montgomery's operations written by Eisenhower's <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_staff" title="Chief of staff">chief of staff</a>, General <a href="/wiki/Walter_Bedell_Smith" title="Walter Bedell Smith">Walter Bedell Smith</a> who met with Montgomery in late June 1944 says nothing about Montgomery conducting a "holding operation" in the Caen sector, and instead speaks of him seeking a "breakout" into the plains south of the Seine.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> On 12 June, Montgomery ordered the 7th Armoured Division into an attack against the <a href="/wiki/Panzer_Lehr_Division" title="Panzer Lehr Division">Panzer Lehr Division</a> that made good progress at first but ended when the Panzer Lehr was joined by the 2nd Panzer Division.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._44_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._44-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> At Villers Bocage on 14 June, the British lost twenty Cromwell tanks to five Tiger tanks led by SS <i>Obersturmführer</i> <a href="/wiki/Michael_Wittmann" title="Michael Wittmann">Michael Wittmann</a> in about five minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._44_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._44-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Despite the setback at Villers Bocage, Montgomery was still optimistic as the Allies were landing more troops and supplies than they were losing in battle, and though the German lines were holding, the <i>Wehrmacht</i> and <i>Waffen SS</i> were suffering considerable attrition.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Air Marshal <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Tedder,_1st_Baron_Tedder" title="Arthur Tedder, 1st Baron Tedder">Sir Arthur Tedder</a> complained that it was impossible to move fighter squadrons to France until Montgomery had captured some airfields, something he asserted that Montgomery appeared incapable of doing.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._47_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._47-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The first V-1 attacks on London, which started on 13 June further increased the pressure on Montgomery from Whitehall to speed up his advance.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._47_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._47-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_British_Army_in_the_Normandy_Campaign_1944_B5634.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/The_British_Army_in_the_Normandy_Campaign_1944_B5634.jpg/220px-The_British_Army_in_the_Normandy_Campaign_1944_B5634.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/The_British_Army_in_the_Normandy_Campaign_1944_B5634.jpg/330px-The_British_Army_in_the_Normandy_Campaign_1944_B5634.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/The_British_Army_in_the_Normandy_Campaign_1944_B5634.jpg/440px-The_British_Army_in_the_Normandy_Campaign_1944_B5634.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="791" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_British_Army_in_the_Normandy_Campaign_1944_B5634.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/George_VI" title="George VI">The King</a> with Lieutenant-General <a href="/wiki/Miles_Dempsey" title="Miles Dempsey">Miles Dempsey</a>, GOC British Second Army, and General Montgomery, 16 June 1944.</div></div></div>
<p>On 18 June, Montgomery ordered Bradley to take Cherbourg while the British were to take Caen by 23 June.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._47_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._47-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> In Operation Epsom, the British VII Corps commanded by Sir <a href="/wiki/Richard_O%27Connor" title="Richard O&#39;Connor">Richard O'Connor</a> attempted to outflank Caen from the west by breaking through the dividing line between the Panzer Lehr and the 12th SS to take the strategic Hill 112.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._48_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._48-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Epsom began well with O'Connor's assault force, the British 15th Scottish Division breaking through and with the 11th Armoured Division stopping the counter-attacks of the 12th SS Division.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._48_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._48-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> General <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Dollmann" title="Friedrich Dollmann">Friedrich Dollmann</a> of the 7th Army had to commit the newly arrived II SS Corps to stop the British offensive.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._48_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._48-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Dollmann, fearing that Epsom would be a success, committed suicide and was replaced by SS <i>Oberstegruppenführer</i> <a href="/wiki/Paul_Hausser" title="Paul Hausser">Paul Hausser</a>. O'Connor, at the cost of about 4,000 men, had won a salient 5 miles (8.0&#160;km) deep and 2 miles (3.2&#160;km) wide but placed the Germans into an unviable long-term position.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._48_103-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._48-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> There was a strong sense of crisis in the Allied command as the Allies had advanced only about 15 miles (24&#160;km) inland, at a time when their plans called for them to have already taken Rennes, Alençon and St. Malo.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._48_103-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._48-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> After Epsom, Montgomery had to tell <a href="/wiki/General" class="mw-redirect" title="General">General</a> <a href="/wiki/Harry_Crerar" title="Harry Crerar">Harry Crerar</a> that the activation of the First Canadian Army would have to wait as there was room at present in the Caen sector only for the newly arrived XII Corps under Lieutenant General <a href="/wiki/Neil_Ritchie" title="Neil Ritchie">Neil Ritchie</a>, which caused some tension with Crerar, who was anxious to get into the field.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Epsom had forced further German forces into Caen but all through June and the first half of July Rommel, Rundstedt, and Hitler were engaged in planning for a great offensive to drive the British into the sea; it was never launched and would have required the commitment of a large number of German forces to the Caen sector.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was only after several failed attempts to break out in the Caen sector that Montgomery devised what he later called his "master plan" of having the 21st Army Group hold the bulk of the German forces, thus allowing the Americans to break out.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The Canadian historians <a href="/wiki/Terry_Copp" title="Terry Copp">Terry Copp</a> and Robert Vogel wrote about the dispute between the "American school" and "British school" after having suffered several setbacks in June 1944:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Montgomery drew what was the indisputably correct conclusion from these events. If the British and Canadians could continue to hold the bulk of the German armoured divisions on their front through a series of limited attacks, they could wear down the Germans and create the conditions for an American breakout on the right.
</p><p>This is what Montgomery proposed in his Directive of June 30th and, if he and his admirers had let the record speak for itself, there would be little debate about his conduct of the first stages of the Normandy campaign. Instead, Montgomery insisted that this Directive was a consistent part of a master plan that he had devised long before the invasion. Curiously, this view does a great disservice to 'Monty' for any rigid planning of operations before the German response was known would have been bad generalship indeed!".<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Hampered by stormy weather and the <a href="/wiki/Bocage" title="Bocage">bocage</a> terrain, Montgomery had to ensure Rommel focused on the British in the east rather than the Americans in the west, who had to take the Cotentin Peninsula and Brittany before the Germans could be trapped by a general swing east.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Montgomery told General Sir <a href="/wiki/Miles_Dempsey" title="Miles Dempsey">Miles Dempsey</a>, the commander of the 2nd British Army: "Go on hitting, drawing the German strength, especially some of the armour, on to yourself – so as to ease the way for Brad [Bradley]."<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._283_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._283-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The Germans had deployed 12 divisions, of which six were Panzer divisions against the British while deploying 8 divisions, of which 3 were Panzer divisions against the Americans.<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._283_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._283-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> By the middle of July Caen had not been taken, as Rommel continued to prioritise prevention of the break-out by British forces rather than the western territories being taken by the Americans.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> This was broadly as Montgomery had planned, albeit not with the same speed as he outlined at St Paul's, although as the American historian Carlo D'Este pointed out the actual situation in Normandy was "vastly different" from what was envisioned at the St. Paul's conference as only one of four goals outlined in May had been achieved by 10 July.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B7767.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B7767.jpg/220px-The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B7767.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B7767.jpg/330px-The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B7767.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B7767.jpg/440px-The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B7767.jpg 2x" data-file-width="796" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B7767.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Prime Minister Churchill with General Montgomery at the latter's HQ in Normandy, July 1944</div></div></div>
<p>On 7 July, Montgomery began Operation Charnwood with a carpet bombing offensive that turned much of the French countryside and the city of Caen into a wasteland.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._53_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._53-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>  The British and Canadians succeeded in advancing into northern Caen before the Germans who used the ruins to their advantage and stopped the offensive.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> On 10 July, Montgomery ordered Bradley to take Avranches, after which the 3rd US Army would be activated to drive towards Le Mans and Alençon.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._56_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._56-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> On 14 July 1944, Montgomery wrote to his patron Brooke, saying he had chosen on a "real show down on the eastern flanks, and to loose a Corps of three armoured divisions in the open country about the Caen-Falaise road...The possibilities are immense; with seven hundred tanks loosed to the South-east of Caen, and the armoured cars operating far ahead, anything can happen."<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/French_Resistance#Role_in_the_liberation_of_France_and_casualties" title="French Resistance">French Resistance had launched Plan Violet</a> in June 1944 to systematically destroy the telephone system of France, which forced the Germans to use their radios more and more to communicate, and as the code-breakers of Bletchley Park had broken many of the German codes, Montgomery had – via Ultra intelligence – a good idea of the German situation.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Montgomery thus knew German Army Group B had lost 96,400 men while receiving 5,200 replacements and the Panzer Lehr Division now based at St. Lô was down to only 40 tanks.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._56_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._56-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Montgomery later wrote that he knew he had the Normandy campaign won at this point as the Germans had almost no reserves while he had three armoured divisions in reserve.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An American break-out was achieved with <a href="/wiki/Operation_Cobra" title="Operation Cobra">Operation Cobra</a> and the encirclement of German forces in the <a href="/wiki/Falaise_pocket" class="mw-redirect" title="Falaise pocket">Falaise pocket</a> at the cost of British losses with the diversionary <a href="/wiki/Operation_Goodwood" title="Operation Goodwood">Operation Goodwood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> On the early morning of 18 July 1944, Operation Goodwood began with British heavy bombers beginning carpet bombing attacks that further devastated what was left of Caen and the surrounding countryside.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> A British tank crewman from the Guards Armoured Division later recalled: "At 0500 hours a distant thunder in the air brought all the sleepy-eyed tank crews out of their blankets. 1,000 Lancasters were flying from the sea in groups of three or four at 3,000 feet (910&#160;m). Ahead of them the pathfinders were scattering their flares and before long the first bombs were dropping".<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> A German tankman from the 21st Panzer Division at the receiving end of this bombardment remembered: "We saw little dots detach themselves from the planes, so many of them that the crazy thought occurred to us: are those leaflets?...Among the thunder of the explosions, we could hear the wounded scream and the insane howling of men who had [been] driven mad".<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The British bombing had badly smashed the German front-line units; e.g. tanks were thrown up on the roofs of French farmhouses. Initially, the three British armoured divisions assigned to lead the offensive, the 7th, 11th and the Guards, made rapid progress and were soon approaching the Borguebus ridge, which dominated the landscape south of Caen by noon.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B6934.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B6934.jpg/220px-The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B6934.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B6934.jpg/330px-The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B6934.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B6934.jpg/440px-The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B6934.jpg 2x" data-file-width="794" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_British_Army_in_Normandy_1944_B6934.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>General Montgomery stops his car to chat to troops during a tour of I Corps area near Caen, 11 July 1944.</div></div></div>
<p>If the British could take the Borguebus ridge, the way to the plains of northern France would be wide open, and potentially Paris could be taken, which explains the ferocity with which the Germans defended the Borguebus ridge. One German officer, Lieutenant Baron von Rosen recalled that to motivate a Luftwaffe officer commanding a battery of four 88&#160;mm guns to fight against the British tanks that he had to hold his handgun to his head "and asked him whether he would like to be killed immediately or get a high decoration. He decided for the latter".<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> The well dug-in 88&#160;mm guns around the Borguebus ridge began taking a toll of the British Sherman tanks and the countryside was soon dotted with dozens of burning Shermans.<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._284_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._284-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> One British officer reported with worry: "I see palls of smoke and tanks brewing up with flames belching forth from their turrets. I see men climbing out, on fire like torches, rolling on the ground to try and douse the flames".<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._284_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._284-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Despite Montgomery's orders to try to press on, fierce German counter-attacks stopped the British offensive.<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._284_124-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._284-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The objectives of Operation Goodwood were all achieved except the complete capture of the Bourgebus Ridge, which was partially taken. The operation was a strategic Allied success in drawing in the last German reserves in Normandy towards the Caen sector away from the American sector, greatly assisting the American break out in Operation Cobra. By the end of Goodwood on 25 July 1944, the Canadians had finally taken Caen while the British tanks had reached the plains south of Caen, giving Montgomery the "hinge" he had been seeking, while forcing the Germans to commit the last of their reserves to stop the Anglo-Canadian offensive.<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._285_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._285-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Ultra decrypts indicated that the Germans now facing Bradley were seriously understrength with Operation Cobra about to commence.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> During Operation Goodwood, the British had 400 tanks knocked out with many recovered returning to service. The casualties were 5,500 with 7 miles (11&#160;km) of ground gained.<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._285_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._285-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Bradley recognised Montgomery's plan to pin down German armour and allow US forces to break out:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The British and Canadian armies were to decoy the enemy reserves and draw them to their front on the extreme eastern edge of the Allied beachhead. Thus, while Monty taunted the enemy at Caen, we [the Americans] were to make our break on the long roundabout road to Paris. When reckoned in terms of national pride, this British decoy mission became a sacrificial one, for while we tramped around the outside flank, the British were to sit in place and pin down the Germans. Yet strategically it fitted into a logical division of labors, for it was towards Caen that the enemy reserves would race once the alarm was sounded.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>The long-running dispute over what Montgomery's "master plan" in Normandy was, led historians to differ greatly about the purpose of Goodwood. The British journalist <a href="/wiki/Mark_Urban" title="Mark Urban">Mark Urban</a> wrote that the purpose of Goodwood was to draw German troops to their left flank to allow the Americans to breakout on the right flank, arguing that Montgomery had to lie to his soldiers about the purpose of Goodwood as the average British soldier would not have understood why they were being asked to create a diversion to allow the Americans to have the glory of staging the breakout with Operation Cobra.<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._285_125-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._285-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> By contrast, the American historian Stephen Power argued that Goodwood was intended to be the "breakout" offensive and not a "holding operation", writing: "It is unrealistic to assert that an operation which called for the use of 4,500 Allied aircraft, 700 artillery pieces and over 8,000 armored vehicles and trucks and that cost the British over 5,500 casualties was conceived and executed for so limited an objective".<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Power noted that Goodwood and Cobra were supposed to take effect on the same day, 18 July 1944, but Cobra was cancelled owing to heavy rain in the American sector, and argued that both operations were meant to be breakout operations to trap the German armies in Normandy. American military writer Drew Middleton wrote that there is no doubt that Montgomery wanted Goodwood to provide a "shield" for Bradley, but at the same time Montgomery was clearly hoping for more than merely diverting German attention away from the American sector.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> British historian <a href="/wiki/John_Keegan" title="John Keegan">John Keegan</a> pointed out that Montgomery made differing statements before Goodwood about the purpose of the operation.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Keegan wrote that Montgomery engaged in what he called a "hedging of his bets" when drafting his plans for Goodwood, with a plan for a "break out if the front collapsed, if not, sound documentary evidence that all he had intended in the first place was a battle of attrition".<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Again Bradley confirmed Montgomery's plan and that the capture of Caen was only incidental to his mission, not critical. The American <i>LIFE</i> magazine quoted Bradley in 1951:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>While Collins was hoisting his VII Corps flag over Cherbourg, Montgomery was spending his reputation in a bitter siege against the old university city of Caen. For three weeks he had rammed his troops against those panzer divisions he had deliberately drawn towards that city as part of our Allied strategy of diversion in the Normandy Campaign. Although Caen contained an important road junction that Montgomery would eventually need, for the moment the capture of that city was only incidental to his mission. For Monty’s primary task was to attract German troops to the British front that we might more easily secure Cherbourg and get into position for the breakout.
</p><p>While this diversion of Monty's was brilliantly achieved, he nevertheless left himself open to criticism by overemphasizing the importance of his thrust toward Caen. Had he limited himself simply to the containment without making Caen a symbol of it, he would have been credited with success instead of being charged, as he was, with failure.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>With Goodwood drawing the Wehrmacht towards the British sector, the First American Army enjoyed a two-to-one numerical superiority with General <a href="/wiki/Omar_Bradley" title="Omar Bradley">Omar Bradley</a> accepting Montgomery's advice to begin the offensive by concentrating at one point instead of a "broad front" as Eisenhower would have preferred.<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._288_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._288-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Operation Goodwood almost cost Montgomery his job, as Eisenhower seriously considered sacking him and only chose not to do so because to sack the popular "Monty" would have caused such a political backlash in Britain against the Americans at a critical moment in the war that the resulting strains in the Atlantic alliance were not considered worth it.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Montgomery expressed his satisfaction at the results of Goodwood when calling the operation off. Eisenhower was under the impression that Goodwood was to be a break out operation. There was a miscommunication between the two men or Eisenhower did not understand the strategy. Alan Brooke chief of the British Imperial General Staff wrote: "Ike knows nothing about strategy and is quite unsuited to the post of Supreme Commander. It is no wonder that Monty's real high ability is not always realised".<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Bradley fully understood Montgomery's intentions. Both men would not give away to the press the true intentions of their strategy.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:General_Montgomery_with_Generals_Patton_(left)_and_Bradley_(centre)_at_21st_Army_Group_HQ,_Normandy,_7_July_1944._B6551.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/General_Montgomery_with_Generals_Patton_%28left%29_and_Bradley_%28centre%29_at_21st_Army_Group_HQ%2C_Normandy%2C_7_July_1944._B6551.jpg/220px-General_Montgomery_with_Generals_Patton_%28left%29_and_Bradley_%28centre%29_at_21st_Army_Group_HQ%2C_Normandy%2C_7_July_1944._B6551.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/General_Montgomery_with_Generals_Patton_%28left%29_and_Bradley_%28centre%29_at_21st_Army_Group_HQ%2C_Normandy%2C_7_July_1944._B6551.jpg/330px-General_Montgomery_with_Generals_Patton_%28left%29_and_Bradley_%28centre%29_at_21st_Army_Group_HQ%2C_Normandy%2C_7_July_1944._B6551.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/General_Montgomery_with_Generals_Patton_%28left%29_and_Bradley_%28centre%29_at_21st_Army_Group_HQ%2C_Normandy%2C_7_July_1944._B6551.jpg/440px-General_Montgomery_with_Generals_Patton_%28left%29_and_Bradley_%28centre%29_at_21st_Army_Group_HQ%2C_Normandy%2C_7_July_1944._B6551.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1772" data-file-height="1781" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:General_Montgomery_with_Generals_Patton_(left)_and_Bradley_(centre)_at_21st_Army_Group_HQ,_Normandy,_7_July_1944._B6551.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>General Montgomery with Lieutenant Generals <a href="/wiki/George_S._Patton" title="George S. Patton">George S. Patton</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Omar_Bradley" title="Omar Bradley">Omar Bradley</a> (centre) at 21st Army Group HQ, 7 July 1944.</div></div></div>
<p>Many American officers had found Montgomery a difficult man to work with, and after Goodwood, pressured Eisenhower to fire Montgomery.<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._285_125-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._285-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Although the Eisenhower–Montgomery dispute is sometimes depicted in nationalist terms as being an Anglo-American struggle, it was the British Air Marshal Arthur Tedder who was pressing Eisenhower most strongly after Goodwood to fire Montgomery.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> An American officer wrote in his diary that Tedder had come to see Eisenhower to "pursue his current favourite subject, the sacking of Monty".<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._287_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._287-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> With Tedder leading the "sack Monty" campaign, it encouraged Montgomery's American enemies to press Eisenhower to fire Montgomery.<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._287_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._287-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Brooke was sufficiently worried about the "sack Monty" campaign to visit Montgomery at his Tactical Headquarters (TAC) in France and as he wrote in his diary; "warned [Montgomery] of a tendency in the PM [Churchill] to listen to suggestions that Monty played for safety and was not prepared to take risks".<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._285_125-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._285-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Brooke advised Montgomery to invite Churchill to Normandy, arguing that if the "sack Monty" campaign had won the Prime Minister over, then his career would be over as having Churchill's backing would give Eisenhower the political "cover" to fire Montgomery.<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._287_139-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._287-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> On 20 July, Montgomery met Eisenhower and on 21 July Churchill at the TAC in France.<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._287_139-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._287-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> One of Montgomery's staff officers wrote afterwards that it was "common knowledge at Tac that Churchill had come to sack Monty".<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._287_139-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._287-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> No notes were taken at the Eisenhower–Montgomery and Churchill–Montgomery meetings, but Montgomery was able to persuade both men not to fire him.<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._288_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._288-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the success of Cobra, which was soon followed by unleashing the 3rd American Army under the General <a href="/wiki/George_S._Patton" title="George S. Patton">George S. Patton</a>, Eisenhower wrote to Montgomery: "Am delighted that your basic plan has begun brilliantly to unfold with Bradley's initial success".<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._289_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._289-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The success of Cobra was aided by Operation Spring when the II Canadian Corps under General <a href="/wiki/Guy_Simonds" title="Guy Simonds">Guy Simonds</a> (the only Canadian general whose skill Montgomery respected) began an offensive south of Caen that made little headway, but which the Germans regarded as the main offensive.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Once the 3rd American Army arrived, Bradley was promoted to take command of the newly created 12th Army Group consisting of 1st and 3rd American Armies. Following the American breakout, there followed the Battle of Falaise Gap as the British, Canadian and Polish soldiers of 21st Army Group commanded by Montgomery advanced south while the American and French soldiers of Bradley's 12th Army Group advanced north to encircle the German Army Group B at Falaise as Montgomery waged what Urban called "a huge battle of annihilation" in August 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._289_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._289-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Montgomery began his offensive into the <i>Suisse Normande</i> region with <a href="/wiki/Operation_Bluecoat" title="Operation Bluecoat">Operation Bluecoat</a> with <a href="/wiki/Richard_O%27Connor" title="Richard O&#39;Connor">Sir Richard O'Connor</a>'s VIII Corps and <a href="/wiki/Gerard_Bucknall" title="Gerard Bucknall">Gerard Bucknall</a>'s XXX Corps heading south.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._73_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._73-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> A dissatisfied Montgomery sacked Bucknall for being insufficiently aggressive and replaced him with General <a href="/wiki/Brian_Horrocks" title="Brian Horrocks">Brian Horrocks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._73_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._73-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> At the same time, Montgomery ordered Patton - whose Third Army was supposed to advance into Brittany - to instead capture <a href="/wiki/Nantes" title="Nantes">Nantes</a>, which was soon taken.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._73_142-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._73-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Hitler waited too long to order his soldiers to retreat from Normandy, leading Montgomery to write: "He [Hitler] refused to face the only sound military course. As a result the Allies caused the enemy staggering losses in men and materials".<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._289_140-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._289-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Knowing via Ultra that Hitler was not planning to retreat from Normandy, Montgomery, on 6 August 1944, ordered an envelopment operation against Army Group B - with the First Canadian Army under Harry Crerar to advance towards Falaise, the Second British Army under Miles Dempsey to advance towards <a href="/wiki/Argentan" title="Argentan">Argentan</a>, and the Third American Army under <a href="/wiki/George_S._Patton" title="George S. Patton">George S. Patton</a> to advance to <a href="/wiki/Alen%C3%A7on" title="Alençon">Alençon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._77_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._77-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> On 11 August, Montgomery changed his plan, with the Canadians to take Falaise and to meet the Americans at Argentan.<sup id="cite_ref-Badsey,_1990_p._77_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Badsey,_1990_p._77-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> The First Canadian Army launched two operations, Operation Totalize on 7 August, which advanced only 9 miles (14&#160;km) in four days in the face of fierce German resistance, and Operation Tractable on 14 August, which finally took Falaise on 17 August.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> In view of the slow Canadian advance, Patton requested permission to take Falaise, but was refused by Bradley on 13 August, which prompted much controversy with many historians arguing that Bradley lacked aggression and that Montgomery should have overruled Bradley.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> The so-called "Falaise Gap" was closed on 22 August 1944, but several American generals, most notably Patton, accused Montgomery of being insufficiently aggressive in closing it, about 60,000 German soldiers were trapped in Normandy, but before 22 August, about 20,000 Germans had escaped through the "Falaise Gap" to fight another day.<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._289_140-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._289-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> About 10,000 Germans had been killed in the Battle of the Falaise Gap, which led a stunned Eisenhower, who viewed the battlefield on 24 August, to comment with horror that it was impossible to walk without stepping on corpses.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> The successful conclusion of the Normandy campaign saw the beginning of the debate between the "American school" and "British school" as both American and British generals started to advance claims about who was most responsible for this victory.<sup id="cite_ref-Urban,_p._289_140-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urban,_p._289-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Brooke wrote in defence of his protégé Montgomery: "Ike knows nothing about strategy and is 'quite' unsuited to the post of Supreme Commander. It is no wonder that Monty's real high ability is not always realised. Especially so when 'national' spectacles pervert the perspective of the strategic landscape".<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> About Montgomery's conduct of the Normandy campaign, Badsey wrote:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Too much discussion on Normandy has centered on the controversial decisions of the Allied commanders. It was not good enough, apparently, to win such a complete and spectacular victory over an enemy that had conquered most of Europe unless it was done perfectly. Most of the blame for this lies with Montgomery, who was foolish enough to insist that it <i>had</i> been done perfectly, that Normandy – and all his other battles – had been fought accordingly to a precise master plan drawn up beforehand, from which he never deviated. It says much for his personality that Montgomery found others to agree with him, despite overwhelmingly evidence to the contrary. His handling of the Battle of Normandy was of a very high order, and as the person who would certainly have been blamed for losing the battle, he deserves the credit for winning it.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advance_to_the_Rhine">Advance to the Rhine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bernard_Montgomery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Advance to the Rhine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018,_Berlin,_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="564" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018,_Berlin,_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Supreme Commanders on 5 June 1945 in Berlin: Bernard Montgomery, <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgy_Zhukov" title="Georgy Zhukov">Georgy Zhukov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean_de_Lattre_de_Tassigny" title="Jean de Lattre de Tassigny">Jean de Lattre de Tassigny</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/General_(United_States)" title="General (United States)">General</a> <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Eisenhower</a> took over Ground Forces Command on 1 September, while continuing as Supreme Commander, with Montgomery continuing to command the 21st Army Group, now consisting mainly of British and Canadian units. Montgomery bitterly resented this change, although it had been agreed before the D-Day invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-weigley_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weigley-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> The British journalist <a href="/wiki/Mark_Urban" title="Mark Urban">Mark Urban</a> writes that Montgomery seemed unable to grasp that as the majority of the 2.2 million Allied soldiers fighting against Germany on the Western Front were now American (the ratio was 3<b>:</b>1) that it was politically unacceptable to American public opinion to have Montgomery remain as Land Forces Commander as: "Politics would not allow him to carry on giving orders to great armies of Americans simply because, in his view, he was better than their generals".<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Winston Churchill had Montgomery promoted to <a href="/wiki/Field_marshal_(United_Kingdom)" title="Field marshal (United Kingdom)">field marshal</a><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> by way of compensation.<sup id="cite_ref-weigley_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weigley-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> In September 1944, Montgomery ordered Crerar and his First Canadian Army to take the French ports on the English Channel, namely Calais, Boulogne and Dunkirk.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp,_1981_p._150_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp,_1981_p._150-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> On 4 September, Antwerp, the third largest port in Europe was captured by Horrocks, with its harbour mostly intact.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Witte_Brigade" title="Witte Brigade">Witte Brigade</a></i> (White Brigade) of the Belgian resistance had captured the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Antwerp" title="Port of Antwerp">Port of Antwerp</a>, before the Germans could destroy the port. Antwerp was a deep water inland port connected to the North Sea via the river <a href="/wiki/Scheldt" title="Scheldt">Scheldt</a>. The Scheldt was wide enough and dredged deep enough to allow the passage of ocean-going ships.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp_&amp;_Vogel_1984_p.129_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp_&amp;_Vogel_1984_p.129-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 3 September 1944 Hitler ordered the 15th German Army, which had been stationed in the Pas de Calais region and was withdrawing north into the Low Countries to hold the mouth of the river Scheldt to deprive the Allies of the use of Antwerp.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp,_1981_p._148_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp,_1981_p._148-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Thanks to ULTRA, Montgomery was aware of Hitler's order by 5 September.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp,_1981_p._148_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp,_1981_p._148-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Starting that same day, SHAEF's naval commander, Admiral Sir <a href="/wiki/Bertram_Ramsay" title="Bertram Ramsay">Bertram Ramsay</a> had urged Montgomery to make clearing the mouth of the Schedlt his number one priority, arguing that as long as the mouth of the Scheldt was in German hands, it was impossible for the Royal Navy to clear the mines in the river, and as the Scheldt was mined, the port of Antwerp was useless.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Alone among the senior commanders, only Ramsay saw opening Antwerp as crucial.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 6 September 1944, Montgomery told Crerar that "I want Boulogne badly" and that city should be taken no matter what the cost.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp,_1981_p._150_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp,_1981_p._150-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> By this point, ports like Cherbourg were too far away from the front line, causing the Allies great logistical problems. The importance of ports closer to Germany was highlighted with the liberation of the city of Le Havre, which was assigned to <a href="/wiki/John_Crocker" title="John Crocker">John Crocker</a>'s I Corps.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp,_1981_p._150_152-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp,_1981_p._150-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> To take Le Havre, two infantry divisions, two tank brigades, most of the artillery of the Second British Army, the specialized armoured "gadgets" of <a href="/wiki/Percy_Hobart" title="Percy Hobart">Percy Hobart</a>'s 79th Armoured Division, the battleship <a href="/wiki/HMS_Warspite_(03)" title="HMS Warspite (03)">HMS&#160;<i>Warspite</i></a> and the monitor <a href="/wiki/HMS_Erebus_(I02)" title="HMS Erebus (I02)">HMS&#160;<i>Erebus</i></a> were all committed.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp,_1981_p._150_152-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp,_1981_p._150-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> On 10 September 1944, Bomber Command dropped 4,719 tons of bombs on Le Havre, which was the prelude to <a href="/wiki/Operation_Astonia" title="Operation Astonia">Operation Astonia</a>, the assault on Le Havre by Crocker's men, which was taken two days later.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp,_1981_p._150_152-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp,_1981_p._150-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> The Canadian historian <a href="/wiki/Terry_Copp" title="Terry Copp">Terry Copp</a> wrote that the commitment of this much firepower and men to take only one French city might "seem excessive", but by this point, the Allies desperately needed ports closer to the front line to sustain their advance.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp,_1981_p._150_152-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp,_1981_p._150-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 9 September, Montgomery wrote to Brooke that "one good Pas de Calais port" would be sufficient to meet all the logistical needs of the 21st Army Group, but only the supply needs of the same formation.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp,_1981_p._150_152-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp,_1981_p._150-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> At the same time, Montgomery noted that "one good Pas de Calais port" would be insufficient for the American armies in France, which thus forced Eisenhower, if for no other reasons than logistics, to favour Montgomery's plans for an invasion of northern Germany by the 21st Army Group, whereas if Antwerp were opened up, then all of the Allied armies could be supplied.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Montgomery ordered that Crerar take Calais, Boulogne and Dunkirk and clear the Scheldt, a task that Crerar stated was impossible as he lacked enough troops to perform both operations at once.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Montgomery refused Crerar's request to have British XII Corps under <a href="/wiki/Neil_Ritchie" title="Neil Ritchie">Neil Ritchie</a> assigned to help clear the Scheldt as Montgomery stated he needed XII Corps for Operation Market Garden.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Montgomery was able to insist that Eisenhower adopt his strategy of a single thrust to the <a href="/wiki/Ruhr_Area" class="mw-redirect" title="Ruhr Area">Ruhr</a> with <a href="/wiki/Operation_Market_Garden" title="Operation Market Garden">Operation Market Garden</a> in September 1944. The offensive was strategically bold.<sup id="cite_ref-Lanning_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lanning-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 22 September 1944, General <a href="/wiki/Guy_Simonds" title="Guy Simonds">Guy Simonds</a>'s II Canadian Corps took Boulogne, followed up by taking Calais on 1 October 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Montgomery was highly impatient with Simonds, complaining that it had taken Crocker's I Corps only two days to take Le Havre while it took Simonds two weeks to take Boulogne and Calais, but Simonds noted that at Le Havre, three divisions and two brigades had been employed whereas as at both Boulogne and Calais, only two brigades were sent in to take both cities.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> After an attempt to storm the <a href="/wiki/Leopold_Canal_(Belgium)" title="Leopold Canal (Belgium)">Leopold Canal</a> by the 4th Canadian Division had been badly smashed by the German defenders, Simonds ordered a stop to further attempts to clear the river Scheldt until his mission of capturing the French ports on the English Channel had been accomplished; this allowed the German 15th Army ample time to dig into its new home on the Scheldt.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> The only port that was not captured by the Canadians was Dunkirk, as Montgomery ordered the 2nd Canadian Division on 15 September to hold his flank at Antwerp as a prelude for an advance up the Scheldt.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp_&amp;_Vogel_1984_p.129_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp_&amp;_Vogel_1984_p.129-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operation_Market_Garden">Operation Market Garden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bernard_Montgomery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Operation Market Garden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Montgomery's plan for <a href="/wiki/Operation_Market_Garden" title="Operation Market Garden">Operation Market Garden</a> (17–25 September 1944) was to outflank the Siegfried Line and cross the Rhine, setting the stage for later offensives into the Ruhr region. The 21st Army Group would attack north from Belgium, 60 miles (97&#160;km) through the Netherlands, across the Rhine and consolidate north of Arnhem on the far side of the Rhine. The risky plan required three Airborne Divisions to capture numerous intact bridges along a single-lane road, on which an entire Corps had to attack and use as its main supply route. The offensive failed to achieve its objectives.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the aftermath of Market Garden, Montgomery made holding the Arnhem salient his first priority, arguing that the 2nd British Army might still be able to break through and reach the wide open plains of northern Germany, and that he might be able to take the Ruhr by the end of October.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> In the meantime, the First Canadian Army, which been given the task of clearing the mouth of the river Scheldt, despite the fact that in the words of Copp and Vogel "...that Montgomery's Directive required the Canadians to continue to fight alone for almost two weeks in a battle which everyone agreed could only be won with the aid of additional divisions".<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> For his part, Field Marshal <a href="/wiki/Gerd_von_Rundstedt" title="Gerd von Rundstedt">Gerd von Rundstedt</a>, the German commander of the Western Front ordered General <a href="/wiki/Gustav-Adolf_von_Zangen" title="Gustav-Adolf von Zangen">Gustav-Adolf von Zangen</a>, the commander of 15th Army that: "The attempt of the enemy to occupy the West Scheldt in order to obtain the free use of the harbor of Antwerp must be <i>resisted to the utmost</i>" (emphasis in the original).<sup id="cite_ref-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.11_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.11-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Rundstedt argued with Hitler that as long as the Allies could not use the port of Antwerp, the Allies would lack the logistical capacity for an invasion of Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.11_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.11-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Montgomery pulled away from the First Canadian Army (temporarily commanded now by Simonds as Crerar was ill), the British 51st Highland Division, 1st Polish Division, British 49th (West Riding) Division and 2nd Canadian Armoured Brigade and sent all of these formations to help the 2nd British Army hold the Arnhem salient.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> However, Simonds seems to have regarded the Scheldt campaign as a test of his ability, and he felt he could clear the Scheldt with only three Canadian divisions, namely the 2nd, the 3rd, and the 4th, despite having to take on the entire 15th Army, which held strongly fortified positions in a landscape that favoured the defence.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.19-20_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.19-20-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Simonds never complained about the lack of air support (made worse by the cloudy October weather), shortages of ammunition or having insufficient troops, regarding these problems as challenges for him to overcome, rather than a cause for complaint.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.19-20_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.19-20-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> As it was, Simonds made only slow progress in October 1944 during the fighting in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Scheldt" title="Battle of the Scheldt">Battle of the Scheldt</a>, although he was praised by Copp for imaginative and aggressive leadership who managed to achieve much, despite all of the odds against him.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Montgomery had little respect for the Canadian generals, whom he dismissed as mediocre, except for Simonds, whom he consistently praised as Canada's only "first-rate" general in the entire war.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp,_1981_p._150_152-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp,_1981_p._150-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Admiral Ramsay, who proved to be a far more articulate and forceful champion of the Canadians than their own generals, starting on 9 October demanded of Eisenhower in a meeting that he either order Montgomery to make supporting the First Canadian Army in the Scheldt fighting his number one priority or sack him.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.42_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.42-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Ramsay in very strong language argued to Eisenhower that the Allies could only invade Germany if Antwerp was opened, and that as long as the three Canadian divisions fighting in the Scheldt had shortages of ammunition and artillery shells because Montgomery made the Arnhem salient his first priority, then Antwerp would not be opened anytime soon.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.42_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.42-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Even Brooke wrote in his diary: "I feel that Monty's strategy for once is at fault. Instead of carrying out the advance to Arnhem he ought to have made certain of Antwerp".<sup id="cite_ref-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.42_172-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.42-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Prompted by Ramsay, Eisenhower sent Montgomery later on 9 October 1944, a cable that emphasizing the "supreme importance of Antwerp", stating that "the Canadian Army will not, repeat not, be able to attack until November unless immediately supplied with adequate ammunition", and finally warning that the Allied advance into Germany would totally stop by mid-November unless Antwerp was opened in October.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.42_172-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.42-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Montgomery replied by accusing Ramsay of making "wild statements" unsupported by the facts, he denied that the Canadians were having to ration ammunition, and claimed that he would soon take the Ruhr, making the Scheldt campaign a sideshow.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.42_172-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.42-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Montgomery further issued a memo entitled "Notes on Command in Western Europe" demanding that he once again be made Land Forces Commander, which led to an exasperated Eisenhower telling Montgomery that the question was not the command arrangement, but rather his ability and willingness to obey orders, and that he either obey orders to clear the mouth of the Scheldt at once, or he would be sacked.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.43_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.43-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A chastised Montgomery told Eisenhower on 15 October 1944 that he was now making clearing the Scheldt his "top priority", and the ammunition shortages in the First Canadian Army, a problem which he denied even existed five days earlier, were now over as supplying the Canadians was henceforth his first concern.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.43_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.43-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Simonds, now reinforced with British troops and Royal Marines, cleared the Scheldt by taking <a href="/wiki/Walcheren" title="Walcheren">Walcheren</a> island, the last of the German "fortresses" on the Scheldt on 8 November 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.127_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.127-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> With the Scheldt in Allied hands, Royal Navy minesweepers removed the German mines in the river, and Antwerp was finally opened to shipping on 28 November 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.127_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.127-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Reflecting Antwerp's importance, the Germans spent the winter of 1944–45 firing V-1 flying bombs and V-2 rockets at it in an attempt to shut down the port, and the German offensive in December 1944 in the Ardennes had as its ultimate objective the capture of Antwerp.<sup id="cite_ref-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.127_174-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copp_&amp;_Vogel,_1985_p.127-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Urban wrote that Montgomery's most "serious failure" in the entire war was not the well publicised Battle of Arnhem, but rather his lack of interest in opening up Antwerp, as without it the entire Allied advance from the North Sea to the Swiss Alps stalled in the autumn of 1944 for logistical reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_the_Bulge">Battle of the Bulge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bernard_Montgomery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Battle of the Bulge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When the surprise attack on the Ardennes took place on 16 December 1944, starting the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Bulge" title="Battle of the Bulge">Battle of the Bulge</a>, the front of the <a href="/wiki/Twelfth_United_States_Army_Group" title="Twelfth United States Army Group">US 12th Army Group</a> was split, with the bulk of the <a href="/wiki/First_United_States_Army" title="First United States Army">US First Army</a> being on the northern shoulder of the German 'bulge'. The 12th Army Group commander, General <a href="/wiki/Omar_Bradley" title="Omar Bradley">Omar Bradley</a>, was located south of the penetration at Luxembourg and command of the US First Army became problematic. Montgomery was the nearest commander on the ground and on 20 December, Eisenhower (who was in <a href="/wiki/Versailles,_Yvelines" title="Versailles, Yvelines">Versailles</a> in France) temporarily transferred <a href="/wiki/Courtney_Hodges" title="Courtney Hodges">Courtney Hodges</a>' US First Army and <a href="/wiki/William_Hood_Simpson" title="William Hood Simpson">William Simpson</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ninth_United_States_Army" title="Ninth United States Army">US Ninth Army</a> to Montgomery's 21st Army Group until the "bulge" could be reduced and a simpler line of communications restored, despite Bradley's vehement objections on national grounds.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;nb 1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When Bradley learned that Montgomery had been given command of two American armies totalling some 200,000 men, he phoned Eisenhower to say: "I cannot be responsible to the American people if you do this. I resign!"<sup id="cite_ref-auto_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-177">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Eisenhower sharply responded that Bradley was in fact responsible to him and "Your resignation means absolutely nothing... Well, Brad, these are my orders."<sup id="cite_ref-auto_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-177">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Montgomery grasped the situation quickly, visiting all <a href="/wiki/Division_(military)" title="Division (military)">divisional</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corps" title="Corps">corps</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Field_army" title="Field army">field army</a> commanders himself and instituting his '<a href="/wiki/GHQ_Liaison_Regiment" title="GHQ Liaison Regiment">Phantom</a>' network of liaison officers. He grouped the <a href="/wiki/XXX_Corps_(United_Kingdom)" title="XXX Corps (United Kingdom)">British XXX Corps</a> as a strategic reserve behind the Meuse and reorganised the US defence of the northern shoulder, shortening and strengthening the line and ordering the evacuation of <a href="/wiki/Sankt_Vith" class="mw-redirect" title="Sankt Vith">St Vith</a>. The German commander of the <a href="/wiki/5th_Panzer_Army" title="5th Panzer Army">5th Panzer Army</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hasso_von_Manteuffel" title="Hasso von Manteuffel">Hasso von Manteuffel</a> said:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The operations of the American First Army had developed into a series of individual holding actions. Montgomery's contribution to restoring the situation was that he turned a series of isolated actions into a coherent battle fought according to a clear and definite plan. It was his refusal to engage in premature and piecemeal counter-attacks which enabled the Americans to gather their reserves and frustrate the German attempts to extend their breakthrough.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>The Wehrmacht's objectives for Betrieb Wacht am Rhein (Operation Watch on the Rhine) was to split the Allied Armies in two by attacking the center of the allied armies through the Ardennes Forest in Belgium (during one of the worst storms in history) and then turning north to recapture the port at Antwerp. On the north-western side of the battle area was Montgomery's 21st Army Group which anchored the northern flank of the allied lines, with Bradley's army group on Montgomery's right flank and Patton's 3rd Army on the far right of Bradley's flank.<sup id="cite_ref-speer459_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-speer459-179">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since <a href="/wiki/SHAEF" class="mw-redirect" title="SHAEF">SHAEF</a> believed the Wehrmacht was no longer capable of launching a major offensive, nor that any offensive could be launched through such rugged terrain as the Ardennes Forest — particularly during winter — the Ardennes was used as an area to send US divisions which had recently fought and sustained severe casualties in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_H%C3%BCrtgen_Forest" title="Battle of Hürtgen Forest">Battle of Hürtgen Forest</a> to regroup and refit. It was also used as a place where new units recently from the US were sent to get some field experience in a <i>safe place</i><b>.</b><sup id="cite_ref-speer459_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-speer459-179">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>General Patton's 3rd Army, which was 90 miles (140&#160;km) to the south, switched from its mission in order to turn north and fought its way through the severe weather and German opposition and broke through to Bastogne, the bad weather cleared so that the <a href="/wiki/USAAF" class="mw-redirect" title="USAAF">USAAF</a> and the RAF<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> could resume air assault operations against <a href="/wiki/Nazi" class="mw-redirect" title="Nazi">Nazi</a> armoured divisions and the <a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">Wehrmacht</a> ran out of petrol.<sup id="cite_ref-speer459_179-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-speer459-179">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is known that the Battle of the Bulge was the largest land battle fought by the western allies during all of World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> This battle is possibly best summed up by <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> in his speech to the House of Commons:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>This is undoubtedly the greatest American battle of the war and will, I believe, be regarded as an ever-famous American victory.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crossing_the_Rhine">Crossing the Rhine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bernard_Montgomery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Crossing the Rhine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
